[2026-02-10T18:57:15Z] Anurag Bhide: Praline: ↵ The word praline is an eponym named after César de Choiseul, Count of Plessis-Praslin, a 17th-century French diplomat and marshal. Legend suggests his personal chef, Clément Lassagne, invented the treat by coating almonds in caramelized sugar to help the Count woo his love interests or appease diplomats. ↵ Originally called "praslines," the recipe traveled to Louisiana with French settlers. There, cooks substituted scarce almonds with local pecans and added cream, creating the iconic American Southern confection.

[2026-02-10T18:58:18Z] Anurag Bhide: Pants / Pantaloons ↵ The word pantaloons derives from Pantalone, a stock character in 16th-century Italian commedia dell’arte. Representing a foolish, elderly Venetian merchant, he was famously depicted wearing tight, ankle-length trousers. By the mid-17th century, the name transitioned from the character to the garment itself. ↵ In the early 19th century, the term was revived for fashionable tight trousers, eventually being shortened to the modern word "pants" by 1840. The name likely traces back to Saint Pantaleon, a popular Venetian patron.

[2026-02-10T18:58:44Z] Anurag Bhide: Nicotine: ↵ Nicotine is named after Jean Nicot, a 16th-century French diplomat. While in Portugal, he became fascinated by tobacco’s supposed medicinal properties and sent seeds to Queen Catherine de' Medici to cure her migraines. ↵ Success in the royal court made the "Queen's Herb" famous. In 1753, Carl Linnaeus officially named the tobacco genus Nicotiana in Nicot’s honor. Finally, when German chemists isolated the alkaloid in 1828, they titled it nicotine, immortalizing the man who introduced it to France.

[2026-02-10T19:03:18Z] Anurag Bhide: Also, ety for mentor ↵ The word mentor originates from Greek mythology. In Homer’s Odyssey, King Odysseus entrusted his household and son, Telemachus, to his friend Mentor before leaving for the Trojan War. During the journey, the goddess Athena often disguised herself as Mentor to provide Telemachus with wise counsel. ↵ The name likely stems from the Proto-Indo-European root *men- (to think). It entered modern English in the 18th century following the popularity of François Fénelon’s novel Les Aventures de Télémaque, which cast Mentor as a central, sagely guide.

[2026-02-10T19:06:56Z] Anurag Bhide: Tarmac ↵ The word tarmac is a portmanteau of "tar" and "macadam." It honors Scottish engineer John Loudon McAdam, who revolutionized road construction in the 1800s by using layers of compacted small stones. ↵ In 1901, Edgar Purnell Hooley noticed a section of road where spilled tar had hardened the dust. He patented a process of pre-coating McAdam’s stones with tar, creating "tar-macadam." Though modern roads use bitumen, the name remains a global shorthand for paved surfaces.

[2026-02-10T19:09:18Z] Anurag Bhide: Mausoleum ↵ The word mausoleum is an eponym derived from Mausolus, a 4th-century BCE ruler of Caria (modern-day Turkey). Upon his death, his widow and sister, Artemisia II, commissioned a magnificent tomb at Halicarnassus that stood nearly 140 feet tall. ↵ Counted among the Seven Wonders of the Ancient World, the Mausoleion was so famous that the Romans began calling all grand, stately tombs "mausolea." By the 15th century, the term entered English to describe any impressive above-ground burial structure.

[2026-02-10T19:12:23Z] Anurag Bhide: Mesmerize ↵ The word mesmerize originates from the name of Franz Anton Mesmer, an 18th-century Austrian physician. Mesmer claimed he could heal patients using an invisible force he called "animal magnetism," involving elaborate ceremonies to induce trancelike states. ↵ Although the scientific community eventually dismissed his theories as fraud, his name became synonymous with the hypnotic effect he achieved. By the early 19th century, the term evolved into "mesmerize," describing the act of holding someone’s attention exclusively and intensely.

[2026-02-10T19:16:51Z] Anurag Bhide: Saxophone ↵ The word saxophone is a combination of a proper name and a Greek suffix. It was coined in the 1840s to honor its inventor, Adolphe Sax, a Belgian instrument maker. He sought to bridge the gap between woodwinds and brass, creating a metal-bodied instrument with a single-reed mouthpiece. ↵ The suffix "-phone" comes from the Greek phōnē, meaning "voice" or "sound." Thus, the name literally translates to "Sax’s sound." It was officially patented in Paris in 1846.

[2026-02-10T19:21:06Z] Anurag Bhide: Macadamia nuts ↵ The word macadamia honors John Macadam, a Scottish-Australian chemist and politician. In 1857, the German-Australian botanist Ferdinand von Mueller officially named the genus Macadamia to commemorate his friend, who served as the secretary of the Philosophical Institute of Victoria. ↵ Ironically, Macadam likely never tasted the nut; he died in 1865 before reaching the trees in person. Before this European naming, Indigenous Australians had enjoyed the native nuts for millennia, calling them Kindal Kindal or Boombera.

[2026-02-11T18:31:57Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: I have 6 questions. Each question will contain three events labeled A, B, and C. Your job is order them in the sequence they happened, from earliest to latest. Let’s say that B occurred first, then C, and then A: the correct answer is BCA. If you get the full sequence right (i.e. BCA in this case), you get +10 points. Before someone else gets BCA, if you get two in the correct order and next to each (i.e., CAB, ABC) you will get +3 points. No negatives for your first wrong answer, but -3 for any wrong answers after that – unless I waive negatives for that question.
